Marshall, Swathi Jha, Sanjay Gupta, Sriram Subramanian\n\nCHI '18: ACM CHI Conference on Human Factors in Computing Systems\nSession: Group collaboration\n\nAbstract\nWe present a study of children collaborating around interactive tabletops in three different countries: India, the United Kingdom and Finland. Our data highlights the key distinctive physical strategies used by children when performing collaborative tasks during this study. Children in India employ dynamic positioning with frequent physical contact and simultaneous object movement. Children in the UK tend to prefer static positioning with minimal physical contact and simultaneous object movement. Children in Finland use a mixture of dynamic and static positioning with minimal physical contact and object movement. Our findings indicate the importance of understanding collaboration strategies and behaviours when designing and deploying interactive tabletops in heterogeneous educational environments. We conclude with a discussion on how designers of tabletops for schools can provide opportunities for children in different countries to define and shape their own collaboration strategies for small group learning that take into account their different classroom practices.\n\nDOI:: https://doi.org/10.1145/3058551\nWEB:: https://chi2018.acm.org/\n\nRecorded at the ACM CHI Conference on Human Use 6TiSCH!\""}, "id": "bgt07r9X510", "statistics": {"likeCount": "0", "dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "46", "favoriteCount": "0"}}], "q": "10.1109/MCOM.2017.1700013"}, {"idx_paper": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1005495", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1H2M40S"}, "snippet": {"channelTitle": "phyloseminar.org", "channelId": "UCbAzhfySv7nLCrNYqZvBSMg", "publishedAt": "2020-02-26T18:09:02Z", "title": "Phyloseminar #94: Don Klinkenberg (Netherlands Nat'l Inst for Public Health & Environment)", "description": "Inferring phylogenetic and transmission trees from genetic sequence data with phybreak\n\nGenetic sequencing of pathogens becomes more and more routine during infectious disease outbreaks. If an outbreak has been closely monitored and all (or most) cases have been sampled, these sequences provide the opportunity to infer who infected whom, which may help in identifying the index case, evaluating outbreak control, and characterising risk factors for infectiousness. However, there is no single path from genetic sequences through a phylogenetic tree to a timed transmission tree, describing who infected whom, and when. In this seminar I will explain how this problem is tackled with phybreak, a model and R package. Phybreak has four submodels, for infection, for sampling, for within-host pathogen dynamics, and for mutation, which together describe the relation between a transmission tree and observed genetic variation. Through an MCMC routine the possible phylogenetic and transmission trees are sampled while integrating over the uncertainty created by all submodels simultaneously. I will cover the original submodels and MCMC procedure (doi.org/10.1371/journal.pcbi.1005495), and show some results obtained with the package. I will finish with some new developments such as multiple samples per host and a wide transmission bottleneck."}, "liveStreamingDetails": {"actualStartTime": "2020-02-26T17:00:20Z"}, "id": "P8byMw_mV1Q", "statistics": {"likeCount": "1", "dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "135", "favoriteCount": "0"}}], "q": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1005495"}, {"idx_paper": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1005535", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT25M58S"}, "snippet": {"description": "An introduction to data analysis with focus on mass-spectrometry proteomics. We investigated whether pattern matching of QRS loops in the 3-dimensional vectorcardiogram (VCG) will improve the grouping of patients whose space-time electrical activity akin to each other, thereby assisting in clinical decision making. First, pattern dissimilarity of VCG QRS loops is qualitatively measured and characterized among patients, resulting in a 93\u00d793 distance matrix of patient-to-patient dissimilarity. Each patient is then represented as a node in the network (or a star in the galaxy), but node locations are optimized to preserve the dissimilarity matrix. The optimization is achieved with a self-organizing algorithm that iteratively minimizes the network energy. Experimental results showed that patients\u2019 locations converge as the representation error reaches a stable phase. The convergence is independent of initial locations of network nodes. Most importantly, 93 patients are automatically organized into 3 clusters of healthy control, LBBB, and infarction. Spatial coordinates of nodes (or patients) are evidently novel predictors that can be used in the computer-assisted detection of cardiac disorders. Self-organizing pattern matching is shown to have strong potentials for large-scale unsupervised learning of patient groups.\nKeywords: Vectorcardiography; QRS waveforms; Pattern matching; Self-organizing network"}, "id": "RKpVpVlHTGM", "statistics": {"likeCount": "0", "dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "29", "favoriteCount": "0"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.ins.2016.09.020"}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.ins.2016.09.020", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT38S"}, "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Hui Yang", "channelId": "UCVf3CXGso_-AXHLAA-JiYwQ", "publishedAt": "2018-02-13T16:25:30Z", "title": "Self-organizing Visualization of Normal and LBBB Subjects", "description": "Hui Yang, Fabio Leonelli,\nSelf-organizing visualization and pattern matching of vectorcardiographic QRS waveforms,\nComputers in Biology and Medicine,\nVolume 79,\n2016,\nPages 1-9,\nISSN 0010-4825,\nhttps://doi.org/10.1016/j.compbiomed.2016.09.020.\n(http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0010482516302463)\nAbstract: QRS morphology is commonly used in the electrocardiographic diagnosis of ventricular depolarization such as left bundle branch block (LBBB) and ventricular septal infarction. JETem 2017", "description": "Patel A, et al. Pneumonia Diagnosed by Point-of-Care Ultrasound. JETem 2017. 2(3):V11-12. https://doi.org/10.21980/J8XP64\n\nHistory of present illness:  A 46-year-old female was sent to the emergency department from clinic with an oxygen saturation of 86%. She endorsed fevers with cough and generalized weakness for one week. She had been evaluated by her primary care provider on day two of illness and was started empirically on Oseltamivir without improvement of her symptoms. The patient arrived febrile, tachycardic, tachypneic, and hypoxic on room air with left-sided crackles on exam.\n\nSignificant findings: Point-of-care ultrasound of the left lower lobe demonstrates lung hepatization, a classic finding for pneumonia. In addition, a shred sign is present with both air bronchograms and focal B-lines\u2014all suggestive of poorly aerated, consolidated lung(1). The patient was started on antibiotics and admitted to the hospital with a diagnosis of community-acquired pneumonia.\n\nDiscussion: With a systematic approach, a skilled clinician can distinguish patterns on point-of-care ultrasound to evaluate for underlying pathology of the lung. Such findings include focal B-lines, an artifact that results from altered fluid-air compositions in the alveoli(2,3).\n\nStudies show, that when referenced with the gold standard of computed tomography, ultrasound is more sensitive than chest X-ray in diagnosing pneumonia. Nazerian et al(4). conducted a 190-patient study in which ultrasound had a higher sensitivity\u201481% compared to a dismal 65% by chest X-ray. Similarly, Cortellaro et al(5). concluded bedside ultrasound (96% sensitivity) is a reliable and superior tool for timely diagnosis of pneumonia compared to chest X-ray (69% sensitivity).\n\nBourcier et al(6). drew conclusions on the effectiveness of ultrasound over chest X-ray on two groups of patients. In the group who exhibited symptoms for less than 24 hours, ultrasound outperformed chest X-ray, where the PPV of ultrasound was 76% and chest X-ray 23%. For those who exhibited symptoms for more than 24 hours, ultrasound demonstrated a PPV of 93% while chest X-ray only 77%. Thus, the use of point-of-care ultrasound in the diagnosis of pneumonia can improve sensitivity while minimizing radiation. \n\nTopics: Pneumonia, ultrasound, dyspnea, shortness of breath, hypoxic, respiratory, pulmonology. \n\nReferences: \n1. The key features are as follows. \n\n\n(1) The descriptions of kinematics and environments (e.g., collision avoidance conditions) are precise. There is none abstraction and none approximation at all. \n(2) We pursue for optimal results, rather that only feasible ones. \n(3) The model ensures the constraint satisfactions between adjacent rough mesh points. On the basis of accurate penalty functions, large-scale constraints are successfully incorporated into the optimization criterion, thus transforming the dynamic optimization problem into a static one with simple bounds on the decision variables. \n(4) A metaheuristic method is adopted as the optimizer. Through this, global optimization ability can be guaranteed. \n(5) This code can be extended to consider various kinds of optimization objectives. Some of these exercises to avoid are commonplace in fitness centers and some exercise classes (e.g. Yoga and Pilates). Knowing how to modify potentially unsafe exercises can help you exercise safely and reduce the risk of spinal fracture with osteoporosis.\n\nWebsite: https://www.pelvicexercises.com.au\n\nExercise 1: Forward Bends\nExercises that involve bending forwards can increase the risk of having a spinal wedge or compression fracture with severe osteoporosis (Vertebral T-score less than \u22122.5 SD). A spinal compression fracture occurs when the vertebra are soft and brittle. Bending forwards compresses the vertebra which can cause them to become squashed and fracture with severely decreased bone density. Exercises such as abdominal curls and deep forward bend in Yoga compress the vertebra together. These exercises are easily modified with to reduce the risk of fracture.\n\nExercise 2: Loaded Forward Bends\nLoaded forward bends further increase the risk of spinal wedge fracture and are therefore unsafe osteoporosis exercises. The risk of osteoporosis fracture increases the further forward you bend and the heavier the weight you lift.\n\nExercise 3:  Spinal Rotation\nYour spinal vertebra rotate during normal movement however some spinal rotation exercises can increase the risk of spinal fracture. Parts of the vertebra are forced against each other when the spine rotates to extreme ranges. Examples of extreme range spinal twisting exercises to avoid with severe osteoporosis include full range lumbar rotation exercises taking the knees right across to the ground and abdominal curl exercises that are combined with spinal rotations.\n\nExercise 4: Direct Spinal Loading\nSome gym exercises for the legs and back involve placing a weighted bar at the top of the spine and across the shoulders. This force on the upper spine travels down through the vertebra and the load can compress soft brittle vertebrae causing a compression fracture. These unsafe osteoporosis exercises are readily modified \n\nIf you're currently doing any of these exercises and you have a high risk of spinal fracture with osteoporosis it's worth stopping these exercises now and discussing whether they're appropriate for your condition with your health professional.\n\n#osteoporosisexercises  #osteoporosis #spinalfracture\n\n\nReferences\nBeck BR1, Daly RM2, Singh MA3, Taaffe DR4. (2017) Exercise and Sports Science Australia (ESSA) position statement on exercise prescription for the prevention and management of osteoporosis. J Sci Med Sport. 2017 May;20(5):438-445. doi: 10.1016/j.jsams.2016.10.001. Accordingly, in many \nparts  of  the  world  increased  methamphetamine  use  has  \nbeen identified as a major public health concern. Treatment \nprograms  for  methamphetamine  have  been  effective  in  \nreducing  and  ceasing  use.  However,  there  are  a  range  of  \npossible   barriers   that   have   prevented   these   programs   \nbeing  widely  adopted  by  methamphetamine  users.  This  \nseminar  presents  findings  from  a  systematic  review  and  \nmeta-analysis  that  examined  the  barriers  to  accessing  \nmeth/amphetamine  treatment  reported  in  peer-reviewed  \nliterature.  Strategies  to  improve  access,  engagement,  and  \nretention in appropriately tailored treatment programs for \namphetamine dependent individuals will be discussed.  \n\n:: About the Speaker\nJesse Young is a Research Fellow and PhD Candidate at the \nCentre  for  Mental  Health,  The  University  of  Melbourne.  \nHe holds adjunct Research Fellow positions at the School \nof Population Health, The University of Western Australia \nand the National Drug Research Institute, Curtin University. \n\nFind the full paper here:\nCumming, C., Troeung, L., Young, J. Written language only began at around 3200 BC, but spoken language has existed long ago.\r\n\r\nLearning by listening is a great way to:\r\n- increases imagination and understanding\r\n- improves your listening skills\r\n- improves your own spoken accent\r\n- learn while on the move\r\n- reduce eye strain\r\n\r\nNow learn the vast amount of general knowledge available on Wikipedia through audio (audio article). You could even learn subconsciously by playing the audio while you are sleeping! If you are planning to listen a lot, you could try using a bone conduction headphone, or a standard speaker instead of an earphone.\r\n\r\nYou can find other Wikipedia audio articles too at:\r\nhttps://www.youtube.com/channel/UCuKfABj2eGyjH3ntPxp4YeQ\r\n\r\nYou can upload your own Wikipedia articles through:\r\nhttps://github.com/nodef/wikipedia-tts\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\"The only true wisdom is in knowing you know nothing.\"\r\n- Socrates\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nSUMMARY\r\n=======\r\nWilliam Whewell  ( HEW-\u0259l; 24 May 1794 \u2013 6 March 1866) was an English polymath, scientist, Anglican priest, philosopher, theologian, and historian of science. He was Master of Trinity College, Cambridge.  In his time as a student there, he achieved distinction in both poetry and mathematics.\nWhat is most often remarked about Whewell is the breadth of his endeavours.  In a time of increasing specialisation, Whewell appears as a vestige of an earlier era when natural philosophers dabbled in a bit of everything. He researched ocean tides (for which he won the Royal Medal), published work in the disciplines of mechanics, physics, geology, astronomy, and economics, while also finding the time to compose poetry, author a Bridgewater Treatise, translate the works of Goethe, and write sermons and theological tracts.  In mathematics, Whewell introduced what is now called the Whewell equation, an equation defining the shape of a curve without reference to an arbitrarily chosen coordinate system.\nOne of Whewell's greatest gifts to science was his wordsmithing.  He often corresponded with many in his field and helped them come up with new terms for their discoveries.  Whewell contributed the terms scientist, physicist, linguistics, consilience, catastrophism, uniformitarianism, and astigmatism amongst others; Whewell suggested the terms electrode, ion, dielectric, anode, and cathode to Michael Faraday.Whewell died in Cambridge in 1866 as a result of a fall from his horse.", "defaultLanguage": "en", "publishedAt": "2018-11-25T01:51:09Z", "title": "William Whewell | Wikipedia audio article", "channelTitle": "wikipedia tts", "defaultAudioLanguage": "en", "channelId": "UCGoNozP_2TZV5hVciGW1y6Q", "tags": ["william whewell", "1794 births", "1866 deaths", "ac with 14 elements", "anglican philosophers", "english economists", "english philosophers", "english scientists", "fellows of the royal society", "giftedness", "historians of science", "members of the club", "philosophers of science", "royal medal winners", "second wranglers", "wikipedia audio article", "learning by listening", "improves your listening skills", "learn while on the move", "reduce eye strain", "text to speech"]}, "id": "mV8V2fnO_8U", "statistics": {"likeCount": "0", "dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "89", "favoriteCount": "0"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.media.2016.10.001"}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.media.2016.12.003", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT6S"}, "snippet": {"channelTitle": "JETem", "channelId": "UC2xvpSRrxTKOzRBPG6cIFBw", "publishedAt": "2017-07-06T23:58:52Z", "title": "Pneumonia, Ultrasound. JETem 2017", "description": "Patel A, et al. Pneumonia Diagnosed by Point-of-Care Ultrasound. JETem 2017. 2(3):V11-12. https://doi.org/10.21980/J8XP64\n\nHistory of present illness:  A 46-year-old female was sent to the emergency department from clinic with an oxygen saturation of 86%. She endorsed fevers with cough and generalized weakness for one week. She had been evaluated by her primary care provider on day two of illness and was started empirically on Oseltamivir without improvement of her symptoms. The patient arrived febrile, tachycardic, tachypneic, and hypoxic on room air with left-sided crackles on exam.\n\nSignificant findings: Point-of-care ultrasound of the left lower lobe demonstrates lung hepatization, a classic finding for pneumonia. In addition, a shred sign is present with both air bronchograms and focal B-lines\u2014all suggestive of poorly aerated, consolidated lung(1). The patient was started on antibiotics and admitted to the hospital with a diagnosis of community-acquired pneumonia.\n\nDiscussion: With a systematic approach, a skilled clinician can distinguish patterns on point-of-care ultrasound to evaluate for underlying pathology of the lung. Such findings include focal B-lines, an artifact that results from altered fluid-air compositions in the alveoli(2,3).\n\nStudies show, that when referenced with the gold standard of computed tomography, ultrasound is more sensitive than chest X-ray in diagnosing pneumonia. Nazerian et al(4). conducted a 190-patient study in which ultrasound had a higher sensitivity\u201481% compared to a dismal 65% by chest X-ray. Similarly, Cortellaro et al(5). concluded bedside ultrasound (96% sensitivity) is a reliable and superior tool for timely diagnosis of pneumonia compared to chest X-ray (69% sensitivity).\n\nBourcier et al(6). drew conclusions on the effectiveness of ultrasound over chest X-ray on two groups of patients. In the group who exhibited symptoms for less than 24 hours, ultrasound outperformed chest X-ray, where the PPV of ultrasound was 76% and chest X-ray 23%. For those who exhibited symptoms for more than 24 hours, ultrasound demonstrated a PPV of 93% while chest X-ray only 77%. Thus, the use of point-of-care ultrasound in the diagnosis of pneumonia can improve sensitivity while minimizing radiation. \n\nTopics: Pneumonia, ultrasound, dyspnea, shortness of breath, hypoxic, respiratory, pulmonology. \n\nReferences: \n1. Written language only began at around 3200 BC, but spoken language has existed long ago.\r\n\r\nLearning by listening is a great way to:\r\n- increases imagination and understanding\r\n- improves your listening skills\r\n- improves your own spoken accent\r\n- learn while on the move\r\n- reduce eye strain\r\n\r\nNow learn the vast amount of general knowledge available on Wikipedia through audio (audio article). If you are planning to listen a lot, you could try using a bone conduction headphone, or a standard speaker instead of an earphone.\r\n\r\nYou can find other Wikipedia audio articles too at:\r\nhttps://www.youtube.com/channel/UCuKfABj2eGyjH3ntPxp4YeQ\r\n\r\nYou can upload your own Wikipedia articles through:\r\nhttps://github.com/nodef/wikipedia-tts\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\"The only true wisdom is in knowing you know nothing.\"\r\n- Socrates\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nSUMMARY\r\n=======\r\nKhetr\u0101n\u012b, or Khetranki, is an Indo-Aryan language of north-eastern Balochistan. It is spoken by the majority of the Khetrans, a Baloch tribe that occupies a hilly tract in the Sulaiman Mountains comprising the whole of Barkhan District as well as small parts of neighbouring Kohlu District to the south-west, and Musakhel District to the north. The ethnic Khetran population found to the east in the Dera Ghazi Khan District of Punjab instead speak Saraiki.Khetrani has grammatical features in common with both Saraiki and with Sindhi, but is not mutually intelligible with either. Khetrani has a relatively small number of Balochi loanwords in its vocabulary.It is likely to have been formerly spoken over a wider area, which has been reduced with the expansion of Pashto from the north and Balochi from the south-east. But what if how well kids cooperate with each other is linked to something beyond just good behavior? Research shows that doing things together, especially in unison, increases feelings of closeness and similarity between people. Scientists at the University of Washington\u2019s Institute for Learning & Brain Sciences wondered if moving in unison could help kids cooperate with each other better. To test this, four-year-olds swung on a swing set either randomly or in unison. And then the kids completed a cooperation game together. It turned out that the kids who swung in unison were actually faster and better at the game! These kids also came up with some clever cooperation strategies that improved their score. Interestingly, moving in unison seems to build connections between people. And scientists think this can make it easier to communicate and cooperate with each other \u2013 even for preschoolers. Give this a try in your own life. Try swinging your kids at the same time or try marching in time to some music. Afterwards, do something cooperative like trying to stand up together back-to-back. Pop on over to the Institute for Learning & Brain Sciences\u2019 Facebook or Twitter pages and let us know what happens!\n\nCheck out the study: Tal-Chen Rabinowitch, Andrew N. By Ben Ford", "description": "In this video I am going to explain my research dissertation, as part of the MSc in Counselling at Bangor University. I have two take home messages, one that is for therapists considering their marketing, and the other is for mental health support websites, particularly those focusing on suicide prevention. \n\nTo find out more about A/B testing, this article explains what it is, and how it can be implemented in website design. https://www.optimizely.com/uk/optimization-glossary/ab-testing/\n\nBelow is an abstract to the study, and references with links. \n\nObjective: To explore the effect of priming stimuli within therapist\u2019s online profiles on participant\u2019s help seeking behaviour. Researchers examined interactions between gender and priming stimuli, and priming effect on participant\u2019s choice of the therapist. Method: 191 undergraduates were exposed to empathy and non-empathy primes, embedded within one of four male and female online therapist profiles. Results: Participants exposed to the empathy, compared to non-empathy prime did not score significantly higher in the General Help Seeking Questionnaire, t (189) = -1.577, (p =.117) or the Barret Lennard Relationship Index, t (189) = -.376, (p = .404). Male participant\u2019s help seeking for suicidal thoughts were significantly affected by therapist gender and priming f (1, 339.52) = 4.62, (p = .037). Priming had an overall effect on choice of therapist X2 (1, N = 191) =5.53a, (p = .019). Implications: Mental health websites should consider their representation of gender and logos, and how that affects\u2019 help seeking behaviour in men. Future studies should incorporate implicit task measures, such as choosing between therapists from a directory list, to further explore the effect of priming and gender combinations on therapist choice and help seeking. \n\nReferences\n\nBarrett-Lennard, G. For the motion estimation and synthesis process, different motion datasets are used that contain the full-body motion of a character, including the motion of its fingers. The motion datasets have been pre-processed for being efficiently handled in real-time estimation process. During the runtime of the application, the system recognizes the actions of the user\u2019s hands and assembles the necessary motion of the character\u2019s fingers. By using a hierarchical Hidden Markov Model (HMM), the system learns the phase of the gestures as well as the progress of the motion. To eliminate the searching process of the most probable motion, prior constraints between segment states were assigned manually. During the runtime of our application, by using a forward HMM algorithm, the system synthesizes the necessary motion of a character\u2019s fingers in real-time. The presented methodology is evaluated both numerically (system performance and estimation rate) and perceptually. The results show that, even when a reduced number of finger gestures are used, the synthesized motion can be described as perceptually consistent with the ground truth motion data.\n\n@article{Mousas20171,\ntitle = \"Real-time performance-driven finger motion synthesis \",\njournal = \"Computers & Graphics \",\nvolume = \"65\",\nnumber = \"\",\npages = \"1 - 11\",\nyear = \"2017\",\ndoi = \"http://doi.org/10.1016/j.cag.2017.03.001\",\nurl = \"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0097849317300304\",\nauthor = \"Christos Mousas and Christos-Nikolaos Anagnostopoulos\",\n}"}, "id": "gYWz0GJl91g", "statistics": {"likeCount": "0", "dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "141", "favoriteCount": "0"}}], "q": "10.1016/j.media.2017.03.001"}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.media.2017.03.001", "items": [{"contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1M"}, "snippet": {"description": "Are animals that look like pigs treated differently in a wild boar social unit? European Journal of Protistology 59, 65\u201374\nhttp://dx.doi.org/10.1016/j.ejop.2017.03.004\n\nZoothamnium pelagicum is the only free-floating species among ~1000 peritrich ciliates that develops its complete life cycle in the open ocean. In the NW Mediterranean Sea, Z. pelagicum was usually associated with ectobiotic bacteria, while in the South Atlantic Ocean was sometimes fouled by the diatom Licmophora. Each colony constituted a radial branch that joined at its base with other colonies to form a lens-shaped pseudocolony of up to 400 zooids. The cilia beat slowly, propelling the expanded pseudocolony in the direction of the concave face. Contraction was triggered by external stimuli (threat) or occurred spontaneously. Frame-by-frame analyses of high-speed camera sequences revealed that during contraction the pseudocolony reduced its diameter 70\u201375% in 3\u20133.2 milliseconds with peak velocity up to 350 mm \u00b7 s-1. The contraction induced a forward jump of 1\u20132 mm that attained a peak speed of 110 mm \u00b7 s-1 (~250 pseudocolony lengths \u00b7 s-1) in 5 milliseconds after onset. This medusa-like locomotion at low Reynolds numbers allowed the pseudocolony to exploit new patches of food resources, as well as to escape from predators. Zoothamnium pelagicum has been able to proliferate in the oligotrophic open ocean, while its sessile counterparts are restricted to eutrophic environments.", "publishedAt": "2017-04-05T12:14:57Z", "Source:\nhttps://www.podbean.com/media/share/pb-y2dr4-ab2767\n\n\nSnippit is made possible by listeners like you.\nPlease help support the podcast:\n\u25ba https://www.patreon.com/snippitscience\u00a0\n\n\u00a0\n\nRecently, the concept of eccentric overload training has been gaining a lot of social media coverage and popularity.\u00a0 This type of training has been around for a while despite its uncommon use in an S&amp;C coach\u2019s toolbox.\n\u00a0\nJournal of Science and Medicine in Sport\nVolume 20, Issue 10,\u00a0October 2017, Pages 943-951\n\u00a0\nSkeletal muscle functional and structural adaptations after eccentric overload flywheel resistance training: a systematic review and meta-analysis\n\u00a0\nSergio\u00a0Maroto-Izquierdo\nDavid\u00a0Garc\u00eda-L\u00f3pez\nRodrigo\u00a0Fernandez-Gonzalo\nOsvaldo C.\u00a0Moreira\nJavier\u00a0Gonz\u00e1lez-Gallego\nJos\u00e9 A.\u00a0de Paz\n\u00a0\nhttps://doi.org/10.1016/j.jsams.2017.03.004\n\u00a0\nAbstract\n\u00a0\nObjectives\nThe purpose of this meta-analysis was to examine the effect of flywheel (FW)\u00a0resistance training\u00a0with Eccentric Overload (FW-EOT) on muscle size and\u00a0functional capacities\u00a0(i.e. strength and power) in athletes and healthy subjects, and to compare FW-induced adaptations with those triggered by traditional resistance exercise interventions.\n\u00a0\nDesign\nA\u00a0systematic\u00a0review and meta-analysis of randomised controlled trials.\n\u00a0\nMethods\nA search of electronic databases [PubMed, MEDLINE (SportDiscus),\u00a0Web of Science,\u00a0Scopus\u00a0and PEDro] was conducted to identify all publications employing FW-EOT up to April 30, 2016. Outcomes were analyzed as continuous outcomes using a\u00a0random effects model\u00a0to calculate a standardized mean difference (SMD) and 95% CI. A total of 9 studies with 276 subjects and 92\u00a0effect sizes\u00a0met the inclusion criteria and were included in the statistical analyses.\n\u00a0\nResults\nThe overall pooled estimate from the main effects analysis was 0.63 (95% CI 0.49\u20130.76) with a significant (p\u00a0&lt;\u00a00.001) Z overall effect of 9.17. No significant heterogeneity (p\u00a0value\u00a0=\u00a00.78) was found. The meta-analysis showed significant differences between FW-EOT vs. conventional resistance training in concentric and eccentric strength, muscle power,\u00a0muscle hypertrophy, vertical jump height and running speed, favoring FW-EOT.\n\u00a0\nConclusions\nThis meta-analysis provides evidence supporting the superiority of FW-EOT, compared with traditional weight-stack exercise, to promote\u00a0skeletal muscle\u00a0adaptations in terms of strength, power and size in healthy subjects and athletes.\n\u00a0\nKeywords\nIso-inertial\nMuscle power\nMuscle strength\nHypertrophy\n\u00a0\n\u00a0\n\n\n\n\n\n\nSnippit is sponsored by\u00a0EliteForm, which brings together cutting edge sports science technologies.\u00a0 Thank you EliteForm\u00a0for making Snippit possible.
